<!-- loading Javascript for using the persistence Service -->
    <script src="/templates/jsp/pb2/js/plugin/thiss-ds.js" nonce="58c538dc3d2d40978310bd750e2cbff6"></script>

    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="58c538dc3d2d40978310bd750e2cbff6">

        function getParameter(parameterName) {
            let result = null,
                tmp = [];
            location.search
                .substr(1)
                .split("&")
                .forEach(function (item) {
                    tmp = item.split("=");
                    if (tmp[0] === parameterName) result = tmp[1];
                });
            return result;
        }

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            queryParamsDelimiter: encodeURIComponent('?'),

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                let redirectURL = getParameter('redirectUri');
                if(redirectURL == null){
                    redirectURL = encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
                }
                if(redirectURL.indexOf('saml_referrer') < 0){
                    redirectURL += encodeURIComponent(((redirectURL.indexOf(this.queryParamsDelimiter) !== -1) ? '&' : '?') + 'saml_referrer');
                }
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + redirectURL;
            },
            retrieveEntity: function () {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // get the results
                            var results = res.data;
                            return resolve(results);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('retrieve entity error: ' + err);
                        });
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage duo to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {doi: doi, entityid: entityId, entityTitle: entityTitle, eventGroupKey: '8ab63660-4e4d-466a-93c1-a570e4f8267f'}

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (let i in allSsoInstitutions) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();
                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err){
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

            function riskifiedBeaconLoad() {
                var store_domain = 'https://advanced.onlinelibrary.wiley.com';
                var session_id = '4EB7339A5C0EF7FC2B07F32DF2499B33';
                var url = ('https:' == document.location.protocol ? 'https://' : 'http://')

                s.async = true;
                s.src = url;
                var x = document.getElementsByTagName('script')[0];
                x.parentNode.insertBefore(s, x);
            }

                window.attachEvent('onload', riskifiedBeaconLoad)
            else

                     <p>State Key Laboratory of New Ceramics and Fine Processing, School of Materials Science and Engineering, Tsinghua University, Beijing, 100084 China</p><a class="moreInfoLink" href="/authored-by/Wang/Kuangyu">Search for more papers by this author</a></div></span><span class="accordion-tabbed__tab-mobile  accordion__closed"><a href="/authored-by/Jin/Haibo" class="author-name accordion-tabbed__control" data-id="a8" data-db-target-for="a8" aria-controls="a8" aria-haspopup="true" id="a8_Ctrl" role="button"><span>Haibo Jin</span><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-section_arrow_d"></i></a><span class="comma-separator">,&nbsp;</span><div class="author-info accordion-tabbed__content" data-db-target-of="a8" aria-labelledby="a8_Ctrl" role="region" id="a8">
                     <p class="author-name">Haibo Jin</p>
                     <p>Beijing Key Laboratory of Construction Tailorable Advanced Functional Materials and Green Applications, School of Materials Science and Engineering, Beijing Institute of Technology, Beijing, 100081 China</p><a class="moreInfoLink" href="/authored-by/Jin/Haibo">Search for more papers by this author</a></div></span><span class="accordion-tabbed__tab-mobile  accordion__closed"><a href="/authored-by/Zhao/Yongjie" class="author-name accordion-tabbed__control" data-id="a9" data-db-target-for="a9" aria-controls="a9" aria-haspopup="true" id="a9_Ctrl" role="button"><span>Yongjie Zhao<i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-mail_outline"></i></span><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-section_arrow_d"></i></a><span class="comma-separator">,&nbsp;</span><div class="author-info accordion-tabbed__content" data-db-target-of="a9" aria-labelledby="a9_Ctrl" role="region" id="a9">
                     <p class="author-type mb-1">Corresponding Author</p>
                     <p class="author-name">Yongjie Zhao</p>

                     </ul>
                     <p>Beijing Key Laboratory of Construction Tailorable Advanced Functional Materials and Green Applications, School of Materials Science and Engineering, Beijing Institute of Technology, Beijing, 100081 China</p>

   <div class="abstract-group ">
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en">
         <h2 id="d10949662" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">Abstract</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            
            <p>Manganese-based phosphate cathodes are promising candidates for developing advanced sodium-ion batteries, primarily driven by their reliable elemental abundance, low toxicity, and desirable cycling performance. However, the cooperative Jahn–Teller effect of Mn<sup>3+</sup> will inevitably lead to structural disorder and irreversible phase transition, thus greatly harming the reversible capacity, rate, and cycling performance. Herein, a stable NASICON-type Na<sub>3</sub>MnHf(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> cathode is demonstrated with a volume variation of 1.9% upon the process of Na<sup>+</sup> extraction/insertion based on the robust Hf─O bond and symmetrical MnO<sub>6</sub> octahedron. Moreover, making full use of the stepwise redox reactions of Mn<sup>2+</sup>/Mn<sup>3+</sup>/Mn<sup>4+</sup>, this cathode reveals excellent cycling stability with a capacity retention of 85.4% after 2500 cycles at 10 C. Matching with commercial hard carbon anodes, the assembled full cell keeps a capacity retention of 92.1% with the Coulombic efficiency close to 100% after 600 cycles at 1 C. The research promises opportunities for the structural amelioration of manganese-based phosphate cathodes toward the application in high-performance sodium-ion batteries.</p>
            </div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adfm202310248-sec-0010">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adfm202310248-sec-0010-title">1 Introduction</h2>
         
         <p>The intermittency of renewable energy technologies, including wind energy, solar photovoltaic, and bioenergy, has raised concerns about unstable electricity supply, thus determining the necessity of grid energy storage.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202310248-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202310248-bib-0001_R_d10949647e462" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - Nat. Mater.">1</a>-<a href="#adfm202310248-bib-0003" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202310248-bib-0003_R_d10949647e462" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - Energy Environ. Sci.">3</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> As the main member of electrochemical energy storage technologies, lithium-ion batteries (LIBs) are strong competitor for deploying renewable energies. However, the ever-increasing cost of lithium resources and safety issues of LIBs have obviously accelerated the research and development (R&amp;D) of sodium-ion batteries (SIBs).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202310248-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202310248-bib-0004_R_d10949647e467" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - Adv. Mater.">4</a>-<a href="#adfm202310248-bib-0006" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202310248-bib-0006_R_d10949647e467" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">6</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, the electrochemical potential of Na<sup>+</sup>/Na is 0.3&nbsp;V higher than that of Li<sup>+</sup>/Li, inherently leading to the lower operation voltage and energy density of sodium ion cathodes.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202310248-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202310248-bib-0007_R_d10949647e477" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - Small">7</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <p>Until then, various cathodes for SIBs have been developed, mainly including Prussian blue analogs, layered transition metal oxides, and polyanionic compounds.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202310248-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202310248-bib-0008_R_d10949647e484" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - Adv. Energy Mater.">8</a>-<a href="#adfm202310248-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202310248-bib-0010_R_d10949647e484" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - Adv. Energy Mater.">10</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Among them, polyanionic compounds have attracted much concern because of their robust structure, high safety, and stable Na<sup>+</sup> diffusive channels.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202310248-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202310248-bib-0011_R_d10949647e491" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - Adv. Energy Mater.">11</a>, <a href="#adfm202310248-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202310248-bib-0012_R_d10949647e491" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - Adv. Sci.">12</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Recently, the sodium super-ionic conductor (NASICON)-type Na<i><sub>x</sub></i>MM′(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> (1 ≤ <i>x</i> ≤ 4, M and M′ represent transition metals) have been widely documented.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202310248-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202310248-bib-0013_R_d10949647e506" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - Adv. Mater.">13</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Some progress has been made in the development of the vanadium-based NASICONs with different combinations of transition metal cations, such as VCr, VAl, VFe, VNi.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202310248-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202310248-bib-0013_R_d10949647e512" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - Adv. Mater.">13</a>-<a href="#adfm202310248-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202310248-bib-0017_R_d10949647e512" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">17</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, low (de)sodiation potential and the underutilized multivalent feature of V weaken the electrochemical advantages of Na<i><sub>x</sub></i>VM(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> cathodes.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202310248-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202310248-bib-0018_R_d10949647e525" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - J. Mater. Chem. A">18</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <p>In comparison, the multi-electron reactions and the elevated (de)sodiation potential of Mn<sup>2+</sup>/Mn<sup>3+</sup>/Mn<sup>4+</sup> make manganese-based NASICON-type competitive candidates as sodium ion cathodes in SIBs. Previously reported NASICON-type Na<sub>3</sub>MnTi(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3,</sub> Na<sub>3</sub>MnZr(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>, and Na<sub>4</sub>MnCr(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> cathodes reveal the redox reactions of Mn<sup>2+</sup>/Mn<sup>3+</sup> (≈3.6&nbsp;V) and Mn<sup>3+</sup>/Mn<sup>4+</sup> (≈4.1&nbsp;V vs Na<sup>+</sup>/Na) couples.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202310248-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202310248-bib-0019_R_d10949647e569" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - Small Methods">19</a>-<a href="#adfm202310248-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202310248-bib-0022_R_d10949647e569" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - Nano Lett.">22</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, the Jahn-Teller (JT) effect of Mn<sup>3+</sup> would cause the large volume variation, irreversible phase transition, and structural disorder, thus deteriorating the reversible capacity and cycling performance.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202310248-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202310248-bib-0023_R_d10949647e576" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - Nat. Sustainability">23</a>, <a href="#adfm202310248-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202310248-bib-0024_R_d10949647e576" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">24</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Previous efforts to suppress JT effect have mainly focused on heteroatom doping/substitution. Magnesium and zirconium elements have been introduced to stabilize the NASICON framework based on the pinning effects; the possible theoretical explanation could be the large ionic radius mismatch which can create the bond-length deviations.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202310248-bib-0025" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202310248-bib-0025_R_d10949647e582" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 25 - Small">25</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, the causal correlation between the local crystal-electronic structure of electrochemically active manganese species in NASICON matrix and the electrochemical performance still remains unrevealed.</p>
         
         <p>Herein, we computationally predicted and successfully synthesized a high-performance NASICON-type manganese-based phosphate sodium ion cathode. Through spectroscopic, structural, and theoretical investigations, it reveals that the Na<sup>+</sup> storage mechanism in Na<sub>3</sub>MnHf(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> cathode includes correlated solid-solution and biphasic reactions. The suppression of the unfavorable JT effect can be demonstrated by the structural stability and small volume change (1.9%) during the electrochemical operation. Moreover, the Na<sub>3</sub>MnHf(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> cathode synthesized by sol–gel method exhibits excellent cycling stability with a capacity retention of 85.4% after 2500 cycles at 10 C (<b>Figure</b> <a href="#adfm202310248-fig-0001">1a</a>). Through providing deep understanding of how to depress JT effect, this work provides some valuable guidance for resolving the structural stability concern in manganese-based polyanionic cathodes.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adfm202310248-fig-0001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/6992d381-8607-4c90-a642-530f0d5ce68d/adfm202310248-fig-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/6992d381-8607-4c90-a642-530f0d5ce68d/adfm202310248-fig-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/1ee7f42e-c61b-4c27-aac3-953cce4afe1e/adfm202310248-fig-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/6992d381-8607-4c90-a642-530f0d5ce68d/adfm202310248-fig-0001-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 1<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202310248-fig-0001&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202310248" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>

      </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adfm202310248-sec-0020">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adfm202310248-sec-0020-title">2 Results and Discussion</h2>
         
         <p>First, based on valence bond theory, we choose hafnium (Hf) element as a dopant composition. The electrochemical activity of the MnHf-based NASICON-structure cathode was theoretically predicted using density functional theory (DFT) calculations. According to the formation energy under different Na<sup>+</sup> concentration, the structural variation during desodiation process is illustrated in the convex-hull phase diagram (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202310248-fig-0001">1b</a>). The formation energy of per formula unit (<i>E</i><sub>f</sub>) for Na<i><sub>x</sub></i>MnHf(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> is also calculated relative to the end member compositions of Na<sub>3</sub>MnHf(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> (<i>E</i><sub>Na3</sub>) and NaMnHf(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> (<i>E</i><sub>Na1</sub>). The results indicate that Na<sub>2</sub>MnHf(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> is the most thermodynamically stable phase. All the metastable phases including intermediate phases of Na<sub>2.5</sub>MnHf(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> and Na<sub>1.5</sub>MnHf(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> could be electrochemically achieved because of the sufficient negative formation energy, illustrating the multi-electron reaction feature of this cathode.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202310248-bib-0026" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202310248-bib-0026_R_d10949647e739" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 26 - ACS Mater. Lett.">26</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In accordance with the lowest-energy structures, the calculated formation energies of various charged states are exhibited in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202310248-fig-0001">1c</a>. When considering the intermediate phase of Na<sub>2.5</sub>MnHf(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> and Na<sub>1.5</sub>MnHf(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>, the electrochemical reactions based on each metastable phase are calculated to occur at 3.39 and 3.856&nbsp;V for the redox reaction of Mn<sup>2+</sup>/Mn<sup>3+</sup> couple, 4.27 and 4.439&nbsp;V for the couple of Mn<sup>3+</sup>/Mn<sup>4+</sup>. Both voltage platforms are higher than that of previously reported Na<sub>3</sub>MnTi(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>, Na<sub>3</sub>MnZr(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>, and Na<sub>4</sub>MnCr(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> cathodes owing to the synergistic effect of Mn and Hf elements.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202310248-bib-0027" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202310248-bib-0027_R_d10949647e789" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 27 - Carbon Neutralization">27</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <p>In addition, Bader charge analysis was introduced to investigate the influence of Hf onto the bonding interaction and the charge transfer upon the charge process. As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202310248-fig-0001">1d</a>, the average Bader charge of O in the MnO<sub>6</sub> octahedra is higher than the HfO<sub>6</sub> octahedra prior to the extraction of Na<sup>+</sup>, which is associated with relatively weak electronegativity of Mn<sup>2+</sup>. With the further extraction of Na<sup>+</sup> (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202310248-fig-0001">1e</a>), the average Bader charge of O in the MnO<sub>6</sub> octahedra increases from −1.501 e (Na<sub>3</sub>MnHf(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>) to −1.352 e (Na<sub>1.5</sub>MnHf(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>), suggesting that O atoms can form strong p-d hybridization with Mn species, acting as the redox centers accompanied with valence change. Meanwhile, functioning as a medium, O atoms can facilitate the transfer of charge between transition metal ions and Na<sup>+</sup>.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202310248-bib-0028" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202310248-bib-0028_R_d10949647e830" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 28 - J. Mater. Chem. A">28</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> As the nearest neighbor atom of O, the average Bader charge of P atom also exhibits a marginal decrease, implying the potential for electron migration along the pathway of TM-O-P-O-TM. Of particular significance, the average Bader charge of O in the HfO<sub>6</sub> octahedra during the charge process reveals no appreciable change, demonstrating that the JT distortion-induced strong strain and structural disorder have been effectively suppressed. The contour maps of charge density on the (110) plane are depicted in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202310248-fig-0001">1f,g</a>. Evidently, the Bader charge of Mn increases while Hf remains almost unchanged. Furthermore, the 3D charge density distribution map in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a> (Supporting Information) also illustrates that with the extraction of Na<sup>+</sup>, the surrounding charge density distribution of Hf tends to be stable and inseparable.</p>
         
         <p>We synthesized the Na<sub>3</sub>MnHf(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> via a typical sol–gel method. The Rietveld refinement of the XRD pattern shows that the Na<sub>3</sub>MnHf(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> crystallizes in a rhombohedral NASICON structure yielding a space group <i>R</i>3c with a satisfactory <i>R</i><sub>wp</sub> value of 8.50% (<b>Figure</b> <a href="#adfm202310248-fig-0002">2a</a>). The unit cell parameters of Na<sub>3</sub>MnHf(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> from the refined profiles are <i>a</i> = 8.939 Å and <i>c</i> = 22.516 Å (Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a>, Supporting Information). These parameters are much larger than those of the well-documented Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> (<i>a</i> = 8.738 Å and <i>c</i> = 21.815 Å) and Na<sub>3</sub>MnTi(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> (<i>a</i> = 8.825 Å and <i>c</i> = 21.708 Å) cathodes for SIBs.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202310248-bib-0029" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202310248-bib-0029_R_d10949647e912" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 29 - Electrochem. Commun.">29</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> This sufficient interstitial volume is favorable for the extraction/insertion of Na<sup>+</sup>. Moreover, in order to prove the intrinsic structural stability, the length of Mn─O bond derived from refined profiles were compared with those of Na<sub>3</sub>MnTi(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> and Na<sub>3</sub>MnZr(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> (Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3</a>, and Tables <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3</a>, Supporting Information). Even in the initial state, the MnO<sub>6</sub> octahedra in these cathodes present different degree of geometric distortion (Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4</a>, Supporting Information). Among them, Na<sub>3</sub>MnHf(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> demonstrates a nearly regular octahedral with an average bond length of 2.089 Å. The carbon content of as-synthesized Na<sub>3</sub>MnHf(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> cathode is identified to be 13.3wt.% by thermo-gravimetric analysis (TGA) (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202310248-fig-0002">2b</a>).</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adfm202310248-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/d28e1d01-7203-4896-b814-16c51a710318/adfm202310248-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/d28e1d01-7203-4896-b814-16c51a710318/adfm202310248-fig-0002-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/bdc907fc-5294-4a0d-b739-c06369d0defc/adfm202310248-fig-0002-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/d28e1d01-7203-4896-b814-16c51a710318/adfm202310248-fig-0002-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">

         </section>
         <p>The morphology of Na<sub>3</sub>MnHf(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> was characterized by transmission electron microscopy (TEM) and scanning electron microscopy (SEM). The Na<sub>3</sub>MnHf(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> powder shows a relatively wide size distribution of 0.2–4&nbsp;µm with a mean size of 0.85&nbsp;µm (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202310248-fig-0002">2c</a>; Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4</a>, Supporting Information). The elaborate combination of the sub-micron and micron particles is beneficial to increase the tap density and mitigate the side reactions at the cathode-electrolyte interface. As indicated in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202310248-fig-0002">2d</a> and Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S5</a> (Supporting Information), the irregular particles are well crystallized. Conformal amorphous carbon layer is tightly coated onto the particle surface, apparently elevating the electron conductivity of the matrix. The <i>I</i><sub>D</sub>/<i>I</i><sub>G</sub> in Raman spectrum is calculated to be 1.05 (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S6</a>, Supporting Information), illustrating the existence of amorphous carbon layer with vacancies and defects. The HR-TEM image displays a lattice fringe of 0.316&nbsp;nm, which can be well indexed with the (024) crystal plane of Na<sub>3</sub>MnHf(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202310248-fig-0002">2e</a>). The typical NASICON hexagonal cell can be seen in the selected area electron diffraction (SAED) pattern, the diffraction dots in the pattern are ascribed to (104),&nbsp;<span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/137e4c43-740e-4b87-9796-e41c903b0785/adfm202310248-math-0002.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:1616301X:media:adfm202310248:adfm202310248-math-0002" display="inline" location="graphic/adfm202310248-math-0002.png">
               <semantics>
                  <mrow>
                     <mo stretchy="false">(</mo>
                     <mn>01</mn>
                     <mover accent="true">
                        <mn>4</mn>
                        <mo>¯</mo>
                     </mover>
                     <mo stretchy="false">)</mo>
                  </mrow>
                  <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">$(01\bar{4})$</annotation>
               </semantics></math>, and (110) planes corresponding to the <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/4de7f964-dd98-47ab-98a5-b8d021f8b54b/adfm202310248-math-0003.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:1616301X:media:adfm202310248:adfm202310248-math-0003" display="inline" location="graphic/adfm202310248-math-0003.png">

                     </mrow>
                     <mo stretchy="false">]</mo>
                  </mrow>
                  <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">$[ {\bar{4}41} ]$</annotation>
               </semantics></math> zone axis (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202310248-fig-0002">2f</a>). The homogeneous distribution of Na, Mn, Hf, P, O, and C elements over the sample is displayed in the TEM elemental mapping (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202310248-fig-0002">2h</a>) results. The XPS spectrum also verifies the successful introduction of Hf species (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S7</a>, Supporting Information). The inductively coupled plasma optical emission spectroscopy (ICP-OES) analysis indicates that the atom ratio of Na: Mn: Hf is 2.97: 1.01: 1, affirming the as-designed chemical formula of Na<sub>3</sub>MnHf(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S8</a>, Supporting Information).</p>
         
         <div class="paragraph-element">The electrochemical performance of Na<sub>3</sub>MnHf(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> cathode was evaluated by cyclic voltammetry (CV) and galvanostatic charge/discharge (GCD) measurement in half-cell with Na metal as the counter and reference electrode. As depicted in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#adfm202310248-fig-0003">3a</a>, two oxidation peaks at 3.7 and 4.2&nbsp;V versus Na<sup>+</sup>/Na are distinctly demonstrated in the CV profiles. These peaks are attributed to the consequent oxidation reaction from Mn<sup>2+</sup> to Mn<sup>3+</sup> and further Mn<sup>4+</sup>, along with the stepwise phase transition from Na<sub>3</sub>MnHf(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> to NaMnHf(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>. Correspondingly, two reduction peaks at 4.0 and 3.4&nbsp;V correspond to the insertion of two Na<sup>+</sup> along with the reduction reaction from Mn<sup>4+</sup> to Mn<sup>2+</sup>. Highly consistent with CV curves, two voltage platforms can be observed in the GCD profiles of Na<sub>3</sub>MnHf(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202310248-fig-0003">3b</a>). The charge plateaus located at 3.6 and 4.2&nbsp;V versus Na<sup>+</sup>/Na are ascribed to the oxidation of Mn<sup>2+</sup> to Mn<sup>3+</sup> and Mn<sup>3+</sup> to Mn<sup>4+</sup>, implying the reversible insertion/extraction of Na<sup>+</sup> in the Na<sub>3</sub>MnHf(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> according to the redox reaction:

                     </semantics></math></span><span class="inline-equation__label">(1)</span></div>
         </div>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adfm202310248-fig-0003"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/95ecc0e1-803e-40d8-b797-855eeeb5983a/adfm202310248-fig-0003-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/95ecc0e1-803e-40d8-b797-855eeeb5983a/adfm202310248-fig-0003-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/12918b2f-2ac3-4a3e-86d9-5e3f796dfb8a/adfm202310248-fig-0003-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/95ecc0e1-803e-40d8-b797-855eeeb5983a/adfm202310248-fig-0003-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 3<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202310248-fig-0003&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202310248" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Electrochemical performance of Na<sub>3</sub>MnHf(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> electrode. a) CV profiles of Na<sub>3</sub>MnHf(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> electrode at the sweep rate of 0.2&nbsp;mV s<sup>−1</sup>. b) Galvanostatic charge/discharge curves of the Na<sub>3</sub>MnHf(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> electrode at a rate of 0.1 C. c,d) Rate performance and corresponding galvanostatic charge and discharge profiles of Na<sub>3</sub>MnHf(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> electrode. e) Capacitance contributions of Na<sub>3</sub>MnHf(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> electrode at the sweep rate of 0.5&nbsp;mV s<sup>−1</sup>. f) Corresponding capacitance contributions at different sweep rate. g) Long cycling performance of the Na<sub>3</sub>MnHf(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> electrode at a charge/discharge rate of 10 C. h) Comparison of the reported NASICON-type phosphate cathodes for sodium batteries.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>These experimental results here are slightly different from the calculated results. Generally, the voltage curve reflects the conversion reactions of electrode materials, including possible phase transitions and disordered or ordered structures, which may lead to the variations of voltage platform.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202310248-bib-0030" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202310248-bib-0030_R_d10949647e1307" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 30 - Phys. Rev. B">30</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The redox plateaus of Na<sub>3</sub>MnHf(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> are apparently higher than vanadium-based NASICON-type cathodes such as Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>1.5</sub>Al<sub>0.5</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> and Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>1.5</sub>Cr<sub>0.5</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> (3.5 and 4.0&nbsp;V vs Na<sup>+</sup>/Na), illustrating the advantage of high operating voltage and energy density.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202310248-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202310248-bib-0014_R_d10949647e1343" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - Energy Storage Mater.">14</a>, <a href="#adfm202310248-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202310248-bib-0017_R_d10949647e1343" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">17</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <p>The rate performance of Na<sub>3</sub>MnHf(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> is displayed in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202310248-fig-0003">3c</a>. At 0.1 C, Na<sub>3</sub>MnHf(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> can deliver a discharge capacity of 90 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>, with an initial Coulombic efficiency (CE) of 77.8%. Additionally, the desirable rate capability of the Na<sub>3</sub>MnHf(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> cathode can be achieved with reversible discharge capacities of 81.2, 77.3, 73.3, 65.1, and 59.3 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at rates of 0.5, 1, 2, 5, and 10 C (1 C = 91.2 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>). When back to 0.1 C, a specific capacity of 81 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> can be recovered. The redox voltage platform at 4.2&nbsp;V versus Na<sup>+</sup>/Na in corresponding GCD profiles could be still maintained even as the specific current gets increased to 10 C (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202310248-fig-0003">3d</a>). Furthermore, excellent long-time cycling stability can be achieved for Na<sub>3</sub>MnHf(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> cathode. The structural stability of the electrode material was evaluated at 1 C for 100 cycles (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S9</a>, Supporting Information). The XRD pattern of Na<sub>3</sub>MnHf(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> electrode after cycling is shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S10</a> (Supporting Information), the characteristic peaks are virtually unchanged in comparison with the pristine electrode. Additionally, the EIS spectra of Na<sub>3</sub>MnHf(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> exhibits a slightly decreased resistance after five cycles, which is attribute to the gradual activation of the cathode. And the resistance gradually increases upon the electrochemical cycling, which can be attributed to the formation of the solid electrolyte interphase (SEI) at the electrolyte-cathode interface (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S11</a>, Supporting Information). Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S12</a> (Supporting Information) shows the cycling performance at 2 C, manifesting a capacity of 69.5 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> after 500 cycles with the capacity retention of 98.6%. The cycling performance at 10 C is also displayed in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202310248-fig-0003">3g</a>. After 2500 cycles, a capacity of 50.6 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> can be retained, rendering 85.4% of the initial capacity.</p>
         
         <p>Encumbered by JT effect of Mn<sup>3+</sup> and unstable solid electrolyte interphase, manganese-based polyanionic cathodes such as Na<sub>3</sub>MnTi(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>, Na<sub>3</sub>MnZr(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>, and Na<sub>4</sub>MnCr(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>, couldn't maintain a steady capacity especially at high rates.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202310248-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202310248-bib-0007_R_d10949647e1451" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - Small">7</a>, <a href="#adfm202310248-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202310248-bib-0021_R_d10949647e1451" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - J. Am. Chem. Soc.">21</a>, <a href="#adfm202310248-bib-0031" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202310248-bib-0031_R_d10949647e1451" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 31 - Adv. Energy Mater.">31</a>-<a href="#adfm202310248-bib-0033" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202310248-bib-0033_R_d10949647e1451" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 33 - Electrochim. Acta">33</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, the Na<sub>3</sub>MnHf(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> exhibits exceptional electrochemical performance within the wide operation voltage region, affirming the effective depression of side reactions and the structural robustness of this cathode (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202310248-fig-0003">3h</a>). The capacity retention and energy densities for this work and other manganese-based polyanionic cathodes are summarized and compared in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S13</a> and Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S5</a> (Supporting Information), indicating the notable advantage of Na<sub>3</sub>MnHf(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> in terms of working voltage. Also, the volumetric density of the cathode could be further promoted by the delicate microstructural engineering such as the introduction of carbon-based additives and the morphology control.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202310248-bib-0034" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202310248-bib-0034_R_d10949647e1479" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 34 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">34</a>-<a href="#adfm202310248-bib-0036" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202310248-bib-0036_R_d10949647e1479" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 36 - Energy Storage Mater.">36</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <p>The Na<sup>+</sup> storage kinetics of Na<sub>3</sub>MnHf(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> cathode was explored via CV analysis with varying sweep rate, and the corresponding <i>b</i>-values are collected in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S14</a> (Supporting Information). Two kinds of energy storage mechanisms are involved as a surface-controlled capacitive process and an intercalation process limited by semi-infinite linear diffusion. The <i>b</i>-values indicate the coexistence of non-Faradaic and Faradaic processes in the electrochemical process of the Na<sub>3</sub>MnHf(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>. Furthermore, the Na<sup>+</sup> storage kinetics is primarily dominated by surface-controlled pseudocapacitive behavior. The quantitative capacitive contribution is also derived from the total current density response of the CV profiles at different sweep rates. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202310248-fig-0003">3e</a> exhibits the proportion of the capacitive controlled specific capacity at 0.5&nbsp;mV s<sup>−1</sup>. The contribution percentage gets increased from 62.21% to 75.07% as the sweep rate gets raised from 0.2 to 0.5&nbsp;mV s<sup>−1</sup> (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202310248-fig-0003">3f</a>). This feature fosters the facilitation of the interfacial diffusion process between the electrode and liquid electrolyte.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202310248-bib-0037" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202310248-bib-0037_R_d10949647e1521" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 37 - ACS Energy Lett.">37</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <p>To probe insight into the phase structure evolution of Na<sub>3</sub>MnHf(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> cathode during the repeated Na<sup>+</sup> extraction/insertion process, in situ XRD analysis was performed during the first cycle at 0.2 C (<b>Figure</b> <a href="#adfm202310248-fig-0004">4a</a>; Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S15</a>, Supporting Information). The cell was first charged to 4.4&nbsp;V and discharged to 2.5&nbsp;V, then recharged to 4.4&nbsp;V versus Na<sup>+</sup>/Na again. Upon the first cycle, all these diffraction peaks experience evident shift and subsequently reverse back to the initial location, demonstrating the high reversibility of phase transition during the cycling process. In particular, the representative (113) and (104) peaks exhibit a continuous rightward shift and gradual reverse back, indicating the lattice contraction and expansion through a single-phase solid-solution reaction. The (300) peak shifts right first and splits into two peaks charging to 3.6&nbsp;V, then continuously shift to higher angle. This new peak appears in the region of 3.6–4.4&nbsp;V indicates the formation of a new phase, suggesting a two-phase reaction process.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202310248-bib-0038" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202310248-bib-0038_R_d10949647e1549" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 38 - Adv. Energy Mater.">38</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> During the sodium storage process, the Na<sub>3</sub>MnHf(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> cathode undergoes the solid-solution and biphasic reactions. Several XRD patterns at the specific charge/discharge stages are selected to further assess the cell parameter evolution during the electrochemical process (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202310248-fig-0004">4b</a>). Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202310248-fig-0004">4c</a> shows the cell parameters of Na<i><sub>x</sub></i>MnHf(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> calculated from the selected XRD patterns. As the charge process continues, the value of lattice constant <i>c</i> is invisibly increased with the decrease of lattice parameter <i>a</i>. According to these related research, loss of Na<sup>+</sup> occurring in the Na(1) position of NASICON structure would result in apparent <i>c</i>-axis elongation.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202310248-bib-0039" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202310248-bib-0039_R_d10949647e1584" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 39 - Solid State Ionics">39</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The inconspicuous increase of lattice parameter <i>c</i> for Na<sub>3</sub>MnHf(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> during the Na<sup>+</sup> extraction also suggests the stabilized occupation of Na(1) site.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202310248-bib-0040" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202310248-bib-0040_R_d10949647e1600" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 40 - Adv. Energy Mater.">40</a>, <a href="#adfm202310248-bib-0041" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202310248-bib-0041_R_d10949647e1600" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 41 - ACS Energy Lett.">41</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Upon cycling, the cathode undertakes a volume variation of 1.9%, which is much smaller than most manganese-based polyanionic cathodes such as Na<sub>3</sub>MnTi(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> (4.8%), Na<sub>4</sub>MnCr(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> (7.7%), and Na<sub>4</sub>Mn<sub>3</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>(P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub>) (7%).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202310248-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202310248-bib-0020_R_d10949647e1631" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - Chem. Mater.">20</a>, <a href="#adfm202310248-bib-0042" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202310248-bib-0042_R_d10949647e1631" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 42 - Adv. Mater.">42</a>, <a href="#adfm202310248-bib-0043" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202310248-bib-0043_R_d10949647e1631" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 43 - Energy Environ. Sci.">43</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The small volume change and marginal alteration of the <i>a</i> and <i>c</i> lattice parameters can be attributed to the robust framework characteristic of Na<sub>3</sub>MnHf(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>, which is key factor for long-life cycling stability of the electrode materials.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adfm202310248-fig-0004"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/8e76a599-e366-40ff-9f89-b7a4b4a76cc4/adfm202310248-fig-0004-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/8e76a599-e366-40ff-9f89-b7a4b4a76cc4/adfm202310248-fig-0004-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/53d8bc17-7311-4670-9c58-85313b20e214/adfm202310248-fig-0004-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/8e76a599-e366-40ff-9f89-b7a4b4a76cc4/adfm202310248-fig-0004-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 4<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202310248-fig-0004&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202310248" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Structural evolution during charging and discharging process. a) The 2D contour plot of in situ XRD of Na<sub>3</sub>MnHf(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> during the first cycle. b,c) In situ XRD patterns at selected electrochemical states and the calculated lattice parameters of Na<sub>3</sub>MnHf(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> during cycle. d) GITT profiles and the apparent Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion coefficient of the Na<sub>3</sub>MnHf(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> cathode cycled at 0.1 C. e–g) The ex situ XPS spectra of Mn 2p, Mn 3s, and Hf 4f of Na<sub>3</sub>MnHf(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> during the charge progress.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>The apparent diffusion kinetic of sodium ion (<i>D</i><sub>Na+</sub>), which is intrinsically related to the electrochemical performance, is also conducted through the galvanostatic intermittent titration technique (GITT) (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202310248-fig-0004">4d</a>), and the detailed calculation process was summarized in Supporting Information and illustrated in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S16</a> (Supporting Information). Evidently, the <i>D</i><sub>Na+</sub> of Na<sub>3</sub>MnHf(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> electrode spans from 10<sup>−14</sup> to 10<sup>−10</sup> cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>−1</sup>, which is comparable to these of other NASICON-structure electrodes.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202310248-bib-0044" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202310248-bib-0044_R_d10949647e1726" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 44 - J. Am. Chem. Soc.">44</a>, <a href="#adfm202310248-bib-0045" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202310248-bib-0045_R_d10949647e1726" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 45 - Adv. Energy Mater.">45</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Notably, The <i>D</i><sub>Na+</sub> is fluctuated with small drops at 3.6&nbsp;V within the bi-phasic reaction region, which is mainly assigned to the retard electrochemical dynamic caused by ordering arrangement of Na<sup>+</sup> and vacancies in NASICON matrix. Previous researches affirm that the Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion kinetics during the single-phase sodiation/desodiation is much faster than that of bi-phasic reaction region.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202310248-bib-0037" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202310248-bib-0037_R_d10949647e1739" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 37 - ACS Energy Lett.">37</a>, <a href="#adfm202310248-bib-0045" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202310248-bib-0045_R_d10949647e1739" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 45 - Adv. Energy Mater.">45</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <p>Furthermore, X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS) analysis was employed to investigate the oxidation state of Mn and Hf at different charge states (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202310248-fig-0004">4e–g</a>). The binding energy centered at 641.2 and 642.4&nbsp;eV of Mn 2p<sub>3/2</sub> for the pristine state suggest the bivalence and trivalence of manganese in as-prepared sample, with the ratio of [Mn<sup>3+</sup>]/([Mn<sup>2+</sup>]+[Mn<sup>3+</sup>]) = 58.49%.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202310248-bib-0040" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202310248-bib-0040_R_d10949647e1758" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 40 - Adv. Energy Mater.">40</a>, <a href="#adfm202310248-bib-0046" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202310248-bib-0046_R_d10949647e1758" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 46 - J. Mater. Chem. A">46</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The peak at ≈647&nbsp;eV is the satellite feature of Mn, which has been documented in previous researches.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202310248-bib-0047" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202310248-bib-0047_R_d10949647e1763" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 47 - J. Electron Spectrosc. Relat. Phenom.">47</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Upon charging to 3.9&nbsp;V, the Mn 2p peak shifts toward a higher binding energy (642.3&nbsp;eV), illustrating the complete oxidation of Mn<sup>2+</sup> to Mn<sup>3+</sup>. The binding energy further increases to 642.7&nbsp;eV at 4.4&nbsp;V, signifying the further transition from Mn<sup>3+</sup> to Mn<sup>4+</sup>. The Mn 3s spectra corresponding to different potentials are also presented in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202310248-fig-0004">4f</a> to corroborate the transition of Na<sub>3</sub>MnHf(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> to NaMnHf(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>. The <i>∆E</i> (5.7&nbsp;eV) between 6&nbsp;eV (<i>∆E</i> for Mn<sup>2+</sup>) and 5.5&nbsp;eV (<i>∆E</i> for Mn<sup>3+</sup>) reconfirms the coexistence of Mn<sup>2+</sup> and Mn<sup>3+</sup>. With the increase of valence state for Mn, the <i>∆E</i> of two featured peaks gradually decreases. Conversely, the binding energy of Hf remains stable throughout the charge process, suggesting that the extraction and insertion of Na<sup>+</sup> are just accompanied with the redox reactions of Mn<sup>2+</sup>/Mn<sup>3+</sup>/Mn<sup>4+</sup>.</p>
         
         <p>The normalized X-ray absorption near edge structure (XANES)&nbsp;spectra at the Mn K-edges provide further analysis of the fine structure and chemical coordination environment of the Mn in Na<sub>3</sub>MnHf(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>. These spectra comprise a pre-edge absorption and two resonances above the absorption edge.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202310248-bib-0048" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202310248-bib-0048_R_d10949647e1826" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 48 - Chem. Mater.">48</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In the XANES spectra (<b>Figure</b> <a href="#adfm202310248-fig-0005">5a</a>), the weak pre-edge peak is ascribed to the transition of a 1<i>s</i> electron to an unoccupied 3<i>d</i> orbital. Due to the dipole selection rule, this transition is intensively forbidden under octahedral symmetry.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202310248-bib-0049" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202310248-bib-0049_R_d10949647e1842" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 49 - Phys. Rev. B">49</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, this transition can be partially allowed on account of 3<i>d</i>–4<i>p</i> orbital mixing, which originates from the slightly distorted MnO<sub>6</sub> octahedral units. The Mn K-edges would shift toward a higher energy during the extraction of Na<sup>+</sup>, signifying the electrochemical activity of Mn upon the electrochemical cycling.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202310248-bib-0038" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202310248-bib-0038_R_d10949647e1856" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 38 - Adv. Energy Mater.">38</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Therefore, the redox reaction of Mn contributes to these two potential plateaus in the charge profile, which is also corroborated by the calculated voltage profiles for the Na<sub>3</sub>MnHf(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202310248-fig-0001">1c</a>).</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adfm202310248-fig-0005"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/4ac207ac-f368-4e22-9700-ba473943e92b/adfm202310248-fig-0005-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/4ac207ac-f368-4e22-9700-ba473943e92b/adfm202310248-fig-0005-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/8206324f-082c-43d5-835a-b2cb16372c0c/adfm202310248-fig-0005-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/4ac207ac-f368-4e22-9700-ba473943e92b/adfm202310248-fig-0005-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 5<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202310248-fig-0005&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202310248" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Analysis of the chemical coordination environment. a) XANES spectra of Mn K-edge, b) Fourier transformed Mn K-edge EXAFS spectra, c–e) CCWT analyses on the Mn K-edge EXAFS signal of Na<sub>3</sub>MnHf(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> at pristine state, 3.8, 4.4&nbsp;V. f–h) Partial density of states of Mn-3d, Hf-5d, and O-2p in Na<sub>3</sub>MnHf(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>, Na<sub>2</sub>MnHf(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>, NaMnHf(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>The extended X-ray absorption fine structure (EXAFS) profiles of Mn species at different electrochemical states are presented in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202310248-fig-0005">5b</a>. For the pristine state, the peak observed at 1.65 Å corresponding to the Mn─O bond with the tendency to split is indicative of the coexistence of Mn<sup>2+</sup> and Mn<sup>3+</sup>. As charged to 3.8&nbsp;V, the peak splits to two with a concomitant reduction in relative intensity, suggesting the JT distortion of active Mn<sup>3+</sup>. This phenomenon can be ascribed to the slight reduction of the symmetry degree of octahedral.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202310248-bib-0037" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202310248-bib-0037_R_d10949647e1926" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 37 - ACS Energy Lett.">37</a>, <a href="#adfm202310248-bib-0042" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202310248-bib-0042_R_d10949647e1926" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 42 - Adv. Mater.">42</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Upon further charging to 4.4&nbsp;V, the relative intensity of Mn─O peak recovers again, implying the restoration of octahedral symmetry and the oxidation of Mn<sup>3+</sup> to Mn<sup>4+</sup>.</p>
         
         <p>To probe the local bonding environment of Mn, the continuous Cauchy Wavelet Transform (CCWT) technology was employed because of its powerful resolution in both <i>K</i> and <i>R</i> spaces. As illustrated in the Wavelet Transform (WT) contour plots of Na<sub>3</sub>MnHf(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202310248-fig-0005">5c–e</a>), the peak at ≈1.8 Å is attributed to the first coordination shell of Mn─O, while the peak at 2.6 Å is related to the Mn─Na bond in the NASICON structure. The relative intensity and length of the Mn─O and Mn─Na bonds exhibit significant diminishment as the depth of charge increases, which are related with gradual extraction of Na<sup>+</sup> and accordingly the variation of locally disordered structure.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202310248-bib-0048" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202310248-bib-0048_R_d10949647e1956" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 48 - Chem. Mater.">48</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <p>The partial density of states (pDOS) of Mn-3<i>d</i>, Hf-5<i>d</i>, and O-2<i>p</i> were calculated to analyze the bonding behavior of Na<sub>3</sub>MnHf(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> during the charge process (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202310248-fig-0005">5f–h</a>). The bandgap of Na<sub>x</sub>MnHf(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> gradually reduces with the extraction of Na<sup>+</sup>, indicating an improved electronic conduction behavior accompanying the charging process. Prior to the extraction of Na<sup>+</sup>, the free electrons in Mn-3<i>d</i> orbitals are tied at the highest energy level (−0.75 to 0&nbsp;eV). The Na<sub>3</sub>MnHf(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> exhibits a significant Mn-3d orbital distribution near the Fermi level, emphasizing the substantial role of Mn atoms in the redox activity. The Mn-3<i>d</i> electrons overlapped with the pDOS of O-2<i>p</i>, demonstrating the strong <i>d-p</i> hybridization between Mn and O orbitals. In addition, the O-2<i>p</i> states exhibit a pronounced overlap with Mn-3<i>d</i> orbitals above the Fermi level in the Na<sub>2</sub>MnHf(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> and NaMnHf(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>, indicating the electron transfer on the Mn-3<i>d</i> state.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202310248-bib-0028" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202310248-bib-0028_R_d10949647e2022" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 28 - J. Mater. Chem. A">28</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Meanwhile, the same behavior is not discernible with Hf (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S17</a>, Supporting Information), implying that Hf does not function as the charge transfer center during the charge process. Additionally, the calculated average bond lengths of Mn─O and Hf─O further certify the aforementioned observations. As is shown, the average Mn─O bond length gets lowered from 2.18 to 1.96 Å along with the extraction of two Na<sup>+</sup>, whereas the Hf─O bond length remains almost unchanged (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S18</a>, Supporting Information). In summary, it is considered that the oxygen in MnO<sub>6</sub> configuration exhibits heightened interaction with its nearest neighboring atoms., which is also confirmed by Bader charge of O coordination configuration (1.501 e/1.352 e vs 1.469 e/1.463 e of MnO<sub>6</sub> and HfO<sub>6</sub>).</p>
         
         <p>For a comprehensive exploration of the interface evolution of Na<sub>3</sub>MnHf(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> cathode in ester-based electrolyte, Time of Flight Secondary Ion Mass Spectrometry (ToF-SIMS) analysis was conducted on the cycled electrode (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S19a</a>, Supporting Information). The presence of NaF<sub>2</sub><sup>−</sup> on the outermost surface of the electrode indicates the formation of cathode electrolyte interface (CEI) film. Corresponding to Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S19b</a> (Supporting Information), the local enrichment is furthermore evident through the overlapping of the NaF<sub>2</sub><sup>−</sup>, which is because of the gap between active cathode powders and the infiltration of liquid electrolyte. However, the MnO<sub>2</sub><sup>−</sup> and HfF<sub>5</sub><sup>−</sup> exhibit a consistent signal intensity distribution over the whole analyzed domain under the CEI film. The normalized signal intensity variation with respect to sputtering time is depicted in in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S19c</a> (Supporting Information). The MnO<sub>2</sub><sup>−</sup> and HfF<sub>5</sub><sup>−</sup> species remain predominantly within the bulk, with negligible migration toward the surface, confirming the structural robustness of NASICON matrix.</p>
         
         <p>To evaluate the practical feasibility, Na<sub>3</sub>MnHf(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> cathode was utilized to match with the hard carbon (HC) anode to assemble full SIBs (<b>Figure</b> <a href="#adfm202310248-fig-0006">6a</a>). The Na<sup>+</sup> storage behavior of hard carbon was assessed, offering a reversible specific capacity of 250 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 1 C in the voltage range of 0.01–3&nbsp;V. The GCD profiles and cycling performance are shown in Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S20</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S21</a> (Supporting Information). The operation voltage window for the full cell was optimized to 2.1–4.0&nbsp;V and the active mass load ratio of cathode and anode was set to 2.5:1 to balance the capacity (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202310248-fig-0006">6b</a>). The rate performance of HC||Na<sub>3</sub>MnHf(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> full cell is shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202310248-fig-0006">6c</a>, which can deliver a specific capacity of 82.5, 70, 63.8, 58.9, and 51.1 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at a current density of 0.1, 0.5, 1, 2, and 5 C (1 C = 91.2 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>). The GCD profiles of the full cell is similar to the cathodic Na<sub>3</sub>MnHf(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>, illustrating the well matching of the HC and Na<sub>3</sub>MnHf(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>. As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202310248-fig-0006">6d</a> and Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S22</a> (Supporting Information), after 600 cycles at 1 C the HC||Na<sub>3</sub>MnHf(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> full cell retains a capacity of 58.6 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> with the capacity retention of 92.1%, and the Coulombic efficiency is close to 100%, signifying the high reversibility of active Na<sup>+</sup>. Summary of rate capability and cycling performance for some polyanionic cathodes in sodium-ion full cells recently reported in the literature is shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202310248-fig-0006">6e</a> and Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S6</a> (Supporting Information). It could be found that Na<sub>3</sub>MnHf(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> demonstrates a more desirable cycling performance with longer cycle life and higher capacity retentions.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adfm202310248-fig-0006"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/33f1eb2f-1d08-4ed8-b008-0508148e7a7a/adfm202310248-fig-0006-m.jpg"><picture>

      <section class="article-section__content" id="adfm202310248-sec-0030">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adfm202310248-sec-0030-title">3 Conclusion</h2>
         
         <p>We investigate the evolution of crystal domains and electronic states of Na<sub>3</sub>MnHf(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> cathode upon electrochemical cycling by combining theoretical calculation with experiment research. The Na<sub>3</sub>MnHf(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> cathode shows a two-electron reaction via the redox of Mn<sup>2+</sup>/Mn<sup>3+</sup> and Mn<sup>3+</sup>/Mn<sup>4+</sup> couples, endowing a reversible capacity of 90&nbsp;mA h g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.1 C and a superior rate capability (a capacity retention of 85.4%, at 10 C and after 2500 cycles). In situ XRD analysis displays a robust framework and small cell volume variation of 1.9% during the process of Na<sup>+</sup> extraction/insertion. The full cell could render a capacity retention of 92.1% after 600 cycles at 1 C. Density functional theory (DFT) calculation provides a feasibility of 5<i>d</i>-Hf doping with the stable Na<sub>2</sub>MnHf(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> intermediate phase and orderly electron configuration. Our findings elucidate that the introduction of Hf can continuously stabilize the local chemical environment of Mn-O upon cycling, thus relieving the cooperative JT distortion.</p>
         </section>
      <div class="article-section__content">
